<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="60" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Mark_Hanna_Voice_Audio.ogg" id="mwe_player_12" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="108811" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (109 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Mark_Hanna_Voice_Audio.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="139696" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c6/Mark_Hanna_Voice_Audio.ogg/Mark_Hanna_Voice_Audio.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">An example of a male with a <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand accent</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Since 1788, English has been spoken in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a> has developed as a first language of the vast majority of the inhabitants of the Australian continent, its standard accent being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Australian" title="General Australian">General Australian</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">English of neighbouring New Zealand</a> has to a lesser degree become an influential standard variety of the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEagleson1982_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEagleson1982-286">[286]</a></sup> Australian and New Zealand English are each other's closest relatives with few differentiating characteristics, followed by <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a> and the English of southeastern England, all of which have similarly non-rhotic accents, aside from some accents in the <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a> of New Zealand. Australian and New Zealand English stand out for their innovative vowels: many short vowels are fronted or raised, whereas many long vowels have diphthongised. Australian English also has a contrast between long and short vowels, not found in most other varieties. Australian English grammar aligns closely to British and American English; like American English, collective plural subjects take on a singular verb (as in <i>the government is</i> rather than <i>are</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200216–21_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200216–21-287">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurridge2010_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurridge2010-288">[288]</a></sup> New Zealand English uses front vowels that are often even higher than in Australian English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200224–26_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200224–26-289">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaclagan2010_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaclagan2010-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordonCampbellHay_et_al.2004_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordonCampbellHay_et_al.2004-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">Philippine English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore_English" title="Singapore English">Singapore English</a></div>
<p>The first significant exposure of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> to the English language occurred in 1762 when the <a href="/wiki/British_occupation_of_Manila" title="British occupation of Manila">British occupied Manila</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>, but this was a brief episode that had no lasting influence. English later became more important and widespread during American rule between 1898 and 1946, and remains an official language of the Philippines. Today, the use of English is ubiquitous in the Philippines, from <a href="/wiki/Road_signs_in_the_Philippines" title="Road signs in the Philippines">street signs</a> and marquees, government documents and forms, courtrooms, the media and entertainment industries, the business sector, and other aspects of daily life. One such usage that is also prominent in the country is in speech, where most <a href="/wiki/Filipinos" title="Filipinos">Filipinos</a> from <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a> would use or have been exposed to <a href="/wiki/Taglish" title="Taglish">Taglish</a>, a form of code-switching between <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a> and English. A similar code-switching method is used by urban native speakers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">Visayan languages</a> called <a href="/wiki/Bislish" title="Bislish">Bislish</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Africa.2C_the_Caribbean.2C_and_South_Asia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa,_the_Caribbean,_and_South_Asia">Africa, the Caribbean, and South Asia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_English" title="Nigerian English">Nigerian English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_English" title="Caribbean English">Caribbean English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_English" title="Pakistani English">Pakistani English</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:South_African_English.ogg" title="File:South African English.ogg">Speech example</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="57" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="South_African_English.ogg" id="mwe_player_13" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="87278" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (87 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/South_African_English.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="139416" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/68/South_African_English.ogg/South_African_English.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">An example of a male with a <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African accent</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>English is spoken widely in southern Africa and is an official or co-official language in several countries. In <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, English has been spoken since 1820, co-existing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrikaans_language" title="Afrikaans language">Afrikaans</a> and various African languages such as the <a href="/wiki/Khoe_languages" title="Khoe languages">Khoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a>. Today, about 9 percent of the South African population speaks <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a> (SAE) as a first language. SAE is a non-rhotic variety, which tends to follow RP as a norm. It is alone among non-rhotic varieties in lacking intrusive r. There are different L2 varieties that differ based on the native language of the speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELanham1982_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELanham1982-292">[292]</a></sup> Most phonological differences from RP are in the vowels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass2002_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass2002-293">[293]</a></sup> Consonant differences include the tendency to pronounce /p, t, t͡ʃ, k/ without aspiration (e.g. <i>pin</i> pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[pɪn]</span> rather than as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[pʰɪn]</span> as in most other varieties), while r is often pronounced as a flap <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> instead of as the more common fricative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200230–31_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200230–31-294">[294]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>

<div class="description">An example of a woman with an educated <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_English" title="Nigerian English">Nigerian accent</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimamanda_Adichie" title="Chimamanda Adichie">Chimamanda Adichie</a>)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Nigerian English is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_dialects_of_the_English_language" title="List of dialects of the English language">dialect of English</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> It is based on British English, but in recent years, because of influence from the United States, some words of American English origin have made it into Nigerian English. Additionally, some new words and collocations have emerged from the language, which come from the need to express concepts specific to the culture of the nation (e.g. <i>senior wife</i>). Over 150 million Nigerians speak English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several varieties of English are also spoken in the Caribbean islands that were colonial possessions of Britain, including Jamaica, and the <a href="/wiki/Leeward_Islands" title="Leeward Islands">Leeward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windward_Islands" title="Windward Islands">Windward Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>. Each of these areas is home both to a local variety of English and a local English-based creole, combining English and African languages. The most prominent varieties are <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_English" title="Jamaican English">Jamaican English</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamaican_English_Creole" title="Jamaican English Creole">Jamaican Creole</a>. In Central America, English-based creoles are spoken in on the Caribbean coasts of Nicaragua and Panama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawton1982_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawton1982-297">[297]</a></sup> Locals are often fluent both in the local English variety and the local creole languages and <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switching</a> between them is frequent, indeed another way to conceptualise the relationship between Creole and Standard varieties is to see a spectrum of social registers with the Creole forms serving as "basilect" and the more RP-like forms serving as the "acrolect", the most formal register.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115-298">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Caribbean varieties are based on British English and consequently, most are non-rhotic, except for formal styles of Jamaican English which are often rhotic. Jamaican English differs from RP in its vowel inventory, which has a distinction between long and short vowels rather than tense and lax vowels as in Standard English. The diphthongs <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ei/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ou/</span> are monophthongs <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eː]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oː]</span> or even the reverse diphthongs <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ie]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[uo]</span> (e.g. <i>bay</i> and <i>boat</i> pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[bʲeː]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[bʷoːt]</span>). Often word-final consonant clusters are simplified so that "child" is pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡ʃail]</span> and "wind" <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[win]</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002117–18_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002117–18-299">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawton1982256–60_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawton1982256–60-300">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115–16_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115–16-301">[301]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>

<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="39" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac" id="mwe_player_15" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="98096" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/80/Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac/Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="221776" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/80/Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac/Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="659575" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="FLAC source" data-title="Original FLAC file (660 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Vandana_Shiva_BBC_Radio4_Saving_Species_23_Dec_2011_b010x8sq.flac" type="audio/flac"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">An example of a <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North Indian</a> woman with an <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian accent</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vandana_Shiva" title="Vandana Shiva">Vandana Shiva</a>)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>As a historical legacy, <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a> tends to take RP as its ideal, and how well this ideal is realised in an individual's speech reflects class distinctions among Indian English speakers. Indian English accents are marked by the pronunciation of phonemes such as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span> (often pronounced with retroflex articulation as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʈ]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɖ]</span>) and the replacement of <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ð/</span> with dentals <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t̪]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d̪]</span>. Sometimes Indian English speakers may also use spelling based pronunciations where the silent ⟨h⟩ found in words such as <i>ghost</i> is pronounced as an Indian <a href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">voiced aspirated</a> stop <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡʱ]</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESailaja200919–24_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESailaja200919–24-302">[302]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span></h2>
<p>Article 1 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a></i> in English:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><i>All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.</i></dd></dl>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19991–56-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991–56_60-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991–56_60-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRomaine1999">Romaine 1999</a>, pp. 1–56.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19992-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19992_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRomaine1999">Romaine 1999</a>, p. 2: "Other changes such as the spread and regularisation of do support began in the thirteenth century and were more or less complete in the nineteenth. Although do coexisted with the simple verb forms in negative statements from the early ninth century, obligatoriness was not complete until the nineteenth. The increasing use of do periphrasis coincides with the fixing of SVO word order. Not surprisingly, do is first widely used in interrogatives, where the word order is disrupted, and then later spread to negatives."</span>
</li>
